<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7028917161117018095">Five-year-old Clara Bynum is dead, drowned in the Potomac River in the shadow of a seemingly haunted rock outcropping known locally as the Three Sisters. River, Cross My Heart, which marks the debut of a wonderfully gifted new storyteller, weighs the effect of Clara's absence on the people she has left behind: her parents, Alice and Willie Bynum, torn between the old world</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7028917161117018095">Five-year-old Clara Bynum is dead, drowned in the Potomac River in the shadow of a seemingly haunted rock outcropping known locally as the Three Sisters. River, Cross My Heart, which marks the debut of a wonderfully gifted new storyteller, weighs the effect of Clara's absence on the people she has left behind: her parents, Alice and Willie Bynum, torn between the old world of their rural North Carolina home and the new world of the city, to which they have moved in search of a better life for themselves and their children; the friends and relatives of the Bynum family in the Georgetown neighborhood they now call home; and, most especially, Clara's sister, ten-year-old Johnnie Mae, who must come to terms with the powerful and confused emotions stirred by her sister's death as she struggles to decide what kind of woman she will become. This highly accomplished first novel resonates with ideas, impassioned lyricism, and poignant historical detail as it captures an essential part of the African-American experience in our century.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7028917161117018095">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_78325527">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78325527" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/151601-tony" class="left imgcol" title="Tony"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1365730769p2/151601.jpg" alt="Tony" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/78325527?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/151601-tony" class="user" title="Tony">Tony</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Jamila</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/151601-tony?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/151601-tony?shelf=project-dcbythebook" class="actionLinkLite">project-dcbythebook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer78325527">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3458075960821658313">The first thing to know about this book is that it basically has no plot -- it's more a series of loosely connected vignettes that, when taken as whole, combine to give the reader an impression of African-American life in the 1920s in a particular neighborhood in Washington, D.C. So, while the book does open with a beautifully rendered chapter in which the 12-year-old protagonist's sister drowns in the Potomac river, that tragedy doesn't lead to the kind of linear story with clear resolution man</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3458075960821658313">The first thing to know about this book is that it basically has no plot -- it's more a series of loosely connected vignettes that, when taken as whole, combine to give the reader an impression of African-American life in the 1920s in a particular neighborhood in Washington, D.C. So, while the book does open with a beautifully rendered chapter in which the 12-year-old protagonist's sister drowns in the Potomac river, that tragedy doesn't lead to the kind of linear story with clear resolution many readers might expect.<br /><br />In that respect, the book is a bit of a failure -- but to my mind, it more than makes up for it by presenting a compelling roster of leading and supporting characters who bring alive the social history of pre-Depression black Washington. To be sure, the little girl's death hovers over the entire book, and the author does a great job of showing how the community rallies to support the family, but it's really about the community, not the tragedy itself. We get little peeks into everyday life, rituals, habits, social mores, and so forth. And of course, racism and it's economic and social consequences are woven throughout the book in a seamless manner.<br /><br />Ultimately, it's a very personal book -- the author lost her child to an accident, and it's hard not to read the book as part of her grieving process. Also, her parents grew up in Georgetown during the era the book describes, and the book began as a story based on their reminisces, so in that sense it honors their history. It's definitely a book worth checking out if you have a connection to Washington, D.C. or just want a good fictional glimpse of African-American social history -- just don't expect much of a story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3458075960821658313">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_86479401">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86479401" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/74688-maya" class="left imgcol" title="Maya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1178113895p2/74688.jpg" alt="Maya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/86479401?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 28, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/74688-maya" class="user" title="Maya">Maya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer86479401">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14486119245513917935">I almost gave River, Cross My Heart one star, but decided that since I actually bothered to finish it, it deserved more than that.<br /><br />The book is about the impact of a little girl's drowning on her family, notably her big sister. It's a bit like a coming of age story, we follow the big sister through her preteen years and see how the family deals with the loss of their youngest daughter as well as the surviving daughter's changing ways. <br /><br />The idea seemed like it could work, but the lack of dialogue ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14486119245513917935">I almost gave River, Cross My Heart one star, but decided that since I actually bothered to finish it, it deserved more than that.<br /><br />The book is about the impact of a little girl's drowning on her family, notably her big sister. It's a bit like a coming of age story, we follow the big sister through her preteen years and see how the family deals with the loss of their youngest daughter as well as the surviving daughter's changing ways. <br /><br />The idea seemed like it could work, but the lack of dialogue made it hard for me to stay focused. There were a lot of random lengthy descriptions of insignificant characters. I feel like if they all were connected to the family in some important way, these descriptions would make sense. But they just seemed out of place, especially the ones that came at the end. <br /><br />Anyway, I wouldn't recommend this one, folks.<br /><br />I would like to share the fact that the book allowed for me to have a very funny interaction with my French-speaking physical therapist to whom I explained the definition of "cross my heart" ! Way to go, Bruno. You got more out of this book than I did.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_73636523">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73636523" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2477905-holly" class="left imgcol" title="Holly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420931812p2/2477905.jpg" alt="Holly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/73636523?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 27, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2477905-holly" class="user" title="Holly">Holly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2477905-holly?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2477905-holly?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2477905-holly?shelf=read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer73636523">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6126086637831138667">I just couldn't get into <a title="River, Cross My Heart (Oprah's Book Club) by Breena Clarke" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/166101.River__Cross_My_Heart__Oprah_s_Book_Club_">River Cross My Heart</a>. The story opens with the tragic death of a young girl, and the following plot always comes back to that scene, and how a family and neighborhood are changed because of the death. I don't know if it is because the reader doesn't <em>know</em> the character that passes away, but because there were so few details I couldn't sympathize with any of the characters, but rather felt that I was reading the news. <br /><br /><a title="Breena Clarke" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/96531.Breena_Clarke">Breena Clarke</a> writes beautifully about 1920's Georgetow</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6126086637831138667">I just couldn't get into <a title="River, Cross My Heart (Oprah's Book Club) by Breena Clarke" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/166101.River__Cross_My_Heart__Oprah_s_Book_Club_">River Cross My Heart</a>. The story opens with the tragic death of a young girl, and the following plot always comes back to that scene, and how a family and neighborhood are changed because of the death. I don't know if it is because the reader doesn't <em>know</em> the character that passes away, but because there were so few details I couldn't sympathize with any of the characters, but rather felt that I was reading the news. <br /><br /><a title="Breena Clarke" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/96531.Breena_Clarke">Breena Clarke</a> writes beautifully about 1920's Georgetown, nearly to an extent that you feel like you're there in the Bynum's kitchen as the smells of Thanksgiving dinner take over the house. I truly commend the writing style of this first novel. <br /><br />The protagonist, Johnnie Mae, lives a troubled life from the point of her sister's death, and many of the scenes with the pool, river, and her friend Pearl left me puzzled. I feel like this book left a lot of things unresolved and walk away somewhat scratching my head. <br /><br />I would give this book a 2.5 star rating. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6126086637831138667">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_128547036">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128547036" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4161080-victoria-smith" class="left imgcol" title="Victoria Smith"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1281982666p2/4161080.jpg" alt="Victoria Smith" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/128547036?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 14, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4161080-victoria-smith" class="user" title="Victoria Smith">Victoria Smith</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/893946.River_Cross_My_Heart" title=" 9780752838199" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer128547036'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer128547036">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4967250593884786747">I am a bit perplexed by this book. On the one hand, it's a beautifully written glimpse into 1920s Georgetown's black community, and the interwoven lives of the various characters. On the other hand, it's a story about a child tragically lost to the Potomac river, and the impact this has upon her parents and older sister, twelve-year-old Johnnie Mae.<br /><br />The overall effect is that it shifts around between styles, neither one thing nor the other. The writing is flawless, but I constantly found that jus</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4967250593884786747">I am a bit perplexed by this book. On the one hand, it's a beautifully written glimpse into 1920s Georgetown's black community, and the interwoven lives of the various characters. On the other hand, it's a story about a child tragically lost to the Potomac river, and the impact this has upon her parents and older sister, twelve-year-old Johnnie Mae.<br /><br />The overall effect is that it shifts around between styles, neither one thing nor the other. The writing is flawless, but I constantly found that just as I was getting into the mind of Johnnie Mae, the perspective would shift and another (often fairly irrelevant) character would be introduced. While it gave a wonderful overview of the community as a whole, I felt that I didn't stay with any one character long enough to really get to know them. I wanted it to be Johnnie Mae's story, and found myself frustrated with the different detours it kept taking. Did Johnnie Mae know that Willie wasn't her real father? Did Willie have some resentment that the child that survived wasn't his? These are things I would have liked to have seen explored in more depth, rather than the arrival of the beautician, or the incident of the injured cat (which I spent the rest of the book wondering about, since it was never followed up).<br /><br />That said, there are some wonderful and/or heartbreaking moments in the book, such as the opening when Clara drowns and Johnnie Mae tries desperately to save her, and when Johnnie Mae and Pearl break into the whites only swimming pool in the middle of the night.<br /><br />It's definitely worth a read if you're interested in black American history, but don't expect much of a plot.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4967250593884786747">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer802795415">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11160532964908181549">I liked the book but did not love it. Perhaps I've read too many southern/racism -oriented books lately and need to take a break, though the topic is always relevant,interesting, and (usually)sad. <br /><br />The author, Breena Clarke, has a lovely, informed voice and is quite eloquent in how she writes, capturing the poor Negro-voice and experience perfectly. The plot, though it held great promise as a poignant story about the accidental death of a child and the impact on those around her, somehow never se</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11160532964908181549">I liked the book but did not love it. Perhaps I've read too many southern/racism -oriented books lately and need to take a break, though the topic is always relevant,interesting, and (usually)sad. <br /><br />The author, Breena Clarke, has a lovely, informed voice and is quite eloquent in how she writes, capturing the poor Negro-voice and experience perfectly. The plot, though it held great promise as a poignant story about the accidental death of a child and the impact on those around her, somehow never seemed to get off the ground. There was far more focus on characters, which would be fine if these characters came together in a cohesive manner and created a captivating story. To me, that just didn't happen and the story never seemed to take flight. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1093949280">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1093949280" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7324976-annie" class="left imgcol" title="Annie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1412720036p2/7324976.jpg" alt="Annie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1093949280?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 31, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7324976-annie" class="user" title="Annie">Annie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1093949280">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3022449186020334967">This is the story of the drowning of little Clara and the tragic event's impact on her family and the Black community in Georgetown, Washington, DC early in the 20th Century. <br />The book has a dream-like quality, captured by the author's writing style of small glimpses into the past as the present unfolds. Johnnie Mae is the main character, the older sister of Clara. Johnnie Mae is present when Clara drowns, escaping into her release of swimming. She is unable to save her little "Rat" and carries t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3022449186020334967">This is the story of the drowning of little Clara and the tragic event's impact on her family and the Black community in Georgetown, Washington, DC early in the 20th Century. <br />The book has a dream-like quality, captured by the author's writing style of small glimpses into the past as the present unfolds. Johnnie Mae is the main character, the older sister of Clara. Johnnie Mae is present when Clara drowns, escaping into her release of swimming. She is unable to save her little "Rat" and carries this burden throughout the story. The descriptions of the many denizens of Georgetown are so well-written, you can almost imagine them, especially the herb woman who lives behind Johnnie Mae's aunt's house and who helps the family on a few occasions, both with her herbal medicines and her wisdom. One of the main focuses of the book is Johnnie Mae's overwhelming desire to swim in the white people's pool, and her dawning understanding of the intractability and absurdity of racism. The pool becomes a sort of hill to die on for Johnnie and leads to a few situations throughout the story. Another piece is the mystical embodiment of the river itself, and water, and the way they call to Johnnie Mae and are such a central part of her being. This slim novel has so many themes it's impossible to describe them all. Highly recommended. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3022449186020334967">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1072826-monique?shelf=back-in-the-day-historical" class="actionLinkLite">back-in-the-day-historical</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer886511294">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2385017980317687992">So just finished this book and had to write this review before I went to sleep..yes I should definitely be asleep LOL however this book is one that is easy to forget about and one you can easily put down and not pick up again (sorry) but, also when you do pick it up and start reading and get into it you can't stop! So it was that I was pleasantly surprised by this book and honestly it would have been a four star book on content and depth alone however it hit a few snags in its chronology but for</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2385017980317687992">So just finished this book and had to write this review before I went to sleep..yes I should definitely be asleep LOL however this book is one that is easy to forget about and one you can easily put down and not pick up again (sorry) but, also when you do pick it up and start reading and get into it you can't stop! So it was that I was pleasantly surprised by this book and honestly it would have been a four star book on content and depth alone however it hit a few snags in its chronology but for its heart it does get a honorable mention :) Also in preliminary news about my reading life, this is the second book that I am reading for a book club--yes sit-at-home-reading-alone and loving-it Nique is trying to broaden her horizons, be a little bit more social and get to know others who love to read as well..baby steps, baby steps but I hopefully will be able to express my thoughts on this book which was essentially about a town in old Georgetown DC focused primarily on an adolescent girl Johnnie Mae and her family in a neighborhood of colored families connected as family pulled together in the most heartbreaking of circumstances--the death of a young child. Left in the care of Johnnie Mae her little sister Clara met her untimely demise in the forbidden and treacherous Potomac river while Johnnie Mae cavorted and relished in her love of the water oblivious to Clara's fear and deadly fall until it was too late..oh the horror when it is discovered that Clara's body went under and now how every member of their community grieves for her and the young life lost and lost opportunities.. this book is memorable in many ways definitely for its scenes of grief and poignancy dealing with the void and absence of a loved one..A standout favorite scene for me is the one where the mother Alice and Johnnie Mae are in the kitchen cooking and the silence from Clara's absence is palpable and real and written so vividly you feel the pain through the pages..I found many scenes like that, anecdotes, side stories and inner thoughts like that very well written and insightful however I did have problems with the overall flow of the story--the flasbacks and time shifts and at times nothing happened, there was little dialogue and just a tad too much description than I like but overall I would recommend this to others and really did like it..Kudos Book Club and the infamous Oprah Book Club too..hmm dare I try more&gt;&gt;:)</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_166674449">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166674449" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5402544-heather" class="left imgcol" title="Heather"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1305037397p2/5402544.jpg" alt="Heather" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/166674449?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 17, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5402544-heather" class="user" title="Heather">Heather</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer166674449'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer166674449">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3476082832817204626">Just some random thoughts...The second half of River, Cross My Heart was more enjoyable than the first 100 pages, which tell of the death of a child. It's important, but less could have been written. Moreover, the main character's coming of age story is unfortunately rushed. I loved Clarke's character descriptions and her illustrations of people's ways. I just wish there was more. For a book with such a sad beginning, it ended on a hopeful note, which was nice. I'd give it more of a 3.5 stars. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer955371275">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11490089238552206347">I gave this book 5 stars for the gifted writing, which so brilliantly tells the story of the devastating loss in the Bynum family. The story gives special focus into the grief of mama, Alice, and older sister, Johnnie Mae, as they deal with the death of their daughter/little sister. Along the way, the reader is introduced to endearing characters; family members, friends, and neighbors, and how they are also affected by this tragic event.<br /><br />I loved the setting of the novel, which gives an interestin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11490089238552206347">I gave this book 5 stars for the gifted writing, which so brilliantly tells the story of the devastating loss in the Bynum family. The story gives special focus into the grief of mama, Alice, and older sister, Johnnie Mae, as they deal with the death of their daughter/little sister. Along the way, the reader is introduced to endearing characters; family members, friends, and neighbors, and how they are also affected by this tragic event.<br /><br />I loved the setting of the novel, which gives an interesting glimpse of a more improved, yet still segregated life of a black community in 1920s Georgetown. <br /><br />The author includes throughout the book some wisdom worth pondering on, such as at the beginning of the novel, when Alice tells Johnnie Mae,"Just go on about your business and hoe the row that God has given you. If looking after your sister is your obligation in life, then be thankful you have a sister."<br /><br />So glad to have found this book at the library.</span>

  <a href="/user/show/19367448-claire---the-coffeeholic-bookworm" class="left imgcol" title="Claire - The Coffeeholic Bookworm"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445472899p2/19367448.jpg" alt="Claire - The Coffeeholic Bookworm" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/850452329?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 18, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19367448-claire---the-coffeeholic-bookworm" class="user" title="Claire - The Coffeeholic Bookworm">Claire - The Coffeeholic Bookworm</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer850452329">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15968596768836047805">Breena Clarke wrote a story that was at first heart-stopping and gut-wrenching, although at the end of the book, it left me hanging and thinking, what happened? I wish the story had had more depth.. I may have to agree with the others, there was no exact plot in the story, it seemed lacking. But her characters, Johnnie Mae particularly, captured my heart, from the moment she realized her sister was gone and felt guilty over her death and for her family's decision to move to another place.<br /><br />This st</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15968596768836047805">Breena Clarke wrote a story that was at first heart-stopping and gut-wrenching, although at the end of the book, it left me hanging and thinking, what happened? I wish the story had had more depth.. I may have to agree with the others, there was no exact plot in the story, it seemed lacking. But her characters, Johnnie Mae particularly, captured my heart, from the moment she realized her sister was gone and felt guilty over her death and for her family's decision to move to another place.<br /><br />This story talks about racial discrimination, too. As young as she was, Johnnie Mae had inquiries that blacks must ask themselves today when stood up to with such a contemptuous thing as bigotry. But that is all it was in the story, that is much as far as it goes, as far as the water from Potomac River flows. I did enjoy reading it, I learned something from Johnnie Mae's struggles as she fulfilled her life's dreams. This may be the reason Oprah once picked this book on her book club.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7313253786218793150">I enjoyed this book! It was centered around a black family in 1920's Georgetown. Johnnie Mae, a pre-teen girl, loses her sister Clara when she drowns in the river. Throughout the book, Johnnie Mae mourns her sister, makes a new friend, Pearl, and goes through growing pains as well as the happiness of getting a new baby brother. This book kind of reminded me of Little House on the Prairie. It was also sad to think of all the struggles the family was going through and the struggles they would continue to face in the future. Loved the ending with Johnnie Mae making accomplishments in her swimming and her parents being so proud of her. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3561641385288705205">This novel reads like a collection of stories where we get to follow a young African American girl named Johnnie Mae as she is coming of age in Georgetown in 1925 and dealing with the accidental drowing of her younger sister Clara. While I wish the author would have given more closure on Clara's death I found the authors way of describing life as an African American in 1925 to be very captivating and emotionally compelling.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText3561641385288705205">This novel reads like a collection of stories where we get to follow a young African American girl named Johnnie Mae as she is coming of age in Georgetown in 1925 and dealing with the accidental drowing of her younger sister Clara. While I wish the author would have given more closure on Clara's death I found the authors way of describing life as an African American in 1925 to be very captivating and emotionally compelling. I still find myself wanting to know more on how Johnnie Maes life turned out and what becomes of her long after the story ends. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1050907694?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 02, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3251235-steph" class="user" title="Steph">Steph</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1050907694">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1678172602935675640">River, Cross My Heart is a quiet, thoughtful story of facing tragedy and its aftermath. Although set in a black community in the early 1900's, Clarke focuses much less on ideas of prejudice and its repercussions on the black community than may be expected. Instead, this is a story about families, about mothers and daughters, about life, death, rebirth, and why it is all worth celebrating. <br /><br />The patchwork intricacies of storytelling that Clarke uses meander back and forth throughout the cast of fri</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1678172602935675640">River, Cross My Heart is a quiet, thoughtful story of facing tragedy and its aftermath. Although set in a black community in the early 1900's, Clarke focuses much less on ideas of prejudice and its repercussions on the black community than may be expected. Instead, this is a story about families, about mothers and daughters, about life, death, rebirth, and why it is all worth celebrating. <br /><br />The patchwork intricacies of storytelling that Clarke uses meander back and forth throughout the cast of friends and relatives, emphasizing the significance of seemingly small happenings. <br /><br />This was an enjoyable read, much like watching the lapping of waves along a calm shoreline or listening to ones grandparents talk about the olden days. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1678172602935675640">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9072651508650432663">I gave this book a 1 out of 5 because it was really confusing and didn't have a family mourn over their daughter's death. This book started off with a family of 4, Willie, Alice, Johnnie Mae, and Clara. Clara died in a river, they had their funeral, then they talked a little bit about how they miss her. Then it goes on and talks about things that happened during their lives after her death, and doesn't really mention her throughout the rest of the story. I got really confused in this book, and I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9072651508650432663">I gave this book a 1 out of 5 because it was really confusing and didn't have a family mourn over their daughter's death. This book started off with a family of 4, Willie, Alice, Johnnie Mae, and Clara. Clara died in a river, they had their funeral, then they talked a little bit about how they miss her. Then it goes on and talks about things that happened during their lives after her death, and doesn't really mention her throughout the rest of the story. I got really confused in this book, and I would not recommend it to anyone. </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer83242964">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10067022763086731207">An interesting family &amp; neighborhood history of Black Georgetown USA in 1925 - racism - segregation etc. Johnnie Mae(10) is responsible for her sister Clara (5) - while Johnnie and friends are swimming in the forbidden Potomac -Clara who was left sitting on a rock drowns. Johnnie Mae doesn't know if she drowned her or let her drown or if she just fell off the rock. Lovely pages about the black church's funeral and all the wisdom of family friends. For instance: the first sentence reads, "Dan</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10067022763086731207">An interesting family &amp; neighborhood history of Black Georgetown USA in 1925 - racism - segregation etc. Johnnie Mae(10) is responsible for her sister Clara (5) - while Johnnie and friends are swimming in the forbidden Potomac -Clara who was left sitting on a rock drowns. Johnnie Mae doesn't know if she drowned her or let her drown or if she just fell off the rock. Lovely pages about the black church's funeral and all the wisdom of family friends. For instance: the first sentence reads, "Dangerous ideas come to life and spread like sparks on dry twigs". Also page 161 another delightful example of the language in this book, "Mama said that everybody matures at her own pace and every girl gets her monthly and develops a bosom. It's just a matter of time. Mama was brusque in her way of talking about these "woman" things. According to her, some subjects aren't worthy of a whole lot of ruminating. The things that make a woman a woman didn't bear much conversation. Only a slatern would fill her days running off at the mouth about what's up underneath people's clothes. A decent woman had too many other things to do with her time. All she said that mornng before school was that Aunt Ina had something for her to try on. So Johnnie Mae must come home right after school let out. (this was a bra that Aund Ina had made for Johnnie)<br />Other than the lovely black language and profound wisdom - this book had no plot. I almost didn't finish the book - kind of slow...</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_26680582">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26680582" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/142955-mary-pellecchia" class="left imgcol" title="Mary Pellecchia"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Mary Pellecchia" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26680582?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 08, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/142955-mary-pellecchia" class="user" title="Mary Pellecchia">Mary Pellecchia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26680582">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16913469682535678148">The star of this book was the setting, 1920's Georgetown, in Washington DC which was a segregated African-American neighborhood in those days. The author pictured well-maintained houses and peaceful, orderly neighborhoods populated by people who at least gave each other the respect and deference denied to them by the outside world. The routineness and viciousness of the racism of the outside world were shocking. The book lets you feel what it is like to live in a world where every single thing,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16913469682535678148">The star of this book was the setting, 1920's Georgetown, in Washington DC which was a segregated African-American neighborhood in those days. The author pictured well-maintained houses and peaceful, orderly neighborhoods populated by people who at least gave each other the respect and deference denied to them by the outside world. The routineness and viciousness of the racism of the outside world were shocking. The book lets you feel what it is like to live in a world where every single thing, every single moment tells you you are poor, inferior, and lucky to be allowed to live. How the people overcome this is the most gratifying part of the story. I got the feeling the author was weaving together stories and anecdotes, with embellishment, that she had heard all her life. The air of authenticity was unmistakable. <br /><br />The seminal event of the book is the drowning of a little girl in the Potomac, the only place she is allowed to swim. The plot shows the effects of this on her parents, but mostly on her older sister who was supposed to be watching her. This was the first book I have read in a long time that had a truly omniscient observer. In fact, it was a little unnerving to move from the sister's thoughts to the mother's, then the father's, then the aunt's, etc. The ending was also a bit unfinished. But I felt transported through history, and I enjoyed that very much. It was uplifting to see the overcoming of trying circumstances. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16913469682535678148">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9472244534156611670">The community welcomed me into the fold on the very first page. Riveting prose captures the young protagonist's loss and the experiences she gains as a result. Focused on family conflicts and racial tension, Clarke illuminates the complexities of children's lives, often overlooked by adults. She portrays strong women who persevere and teach youngsters to do the same. (My mother gave it a big smiley face -- the highest accolade on her rating system.)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6608655100649004917">There are some things about this book that I really enjoyed.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText6608655100649004917">There are some things about this book that I really enjoyed. For example, the description of what is was like to be African American and living in Georgetown in 1925 are stark and emotionally compelling. And Clarke's use of language can be sweetly lyrical. Yet, I feel that there is some element missing from the main story line and, in particular, the portrayal of how Johnnie Mae seeks to come to terms with her sister's drowning. Perhaps it is a gritty depth of characters that the novel seems to demand. Still, an encouraging debut novel.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5473925416613030952">(FROM JACKET)Six-year-old Clara Bynum is dead, drowned in the Potomac River in the shadow of an apparently haunted rock outcropping known locally as the Three Sisters.<br />In scenes alive with emotional truth, "River, Cross My Heart" weighs the effect of Clara's absence on the people she has left behind: her parents, Alice and Willie Bynum, torn between the old world of their rural North Carolina home and the new world of the city, to which they have moved in search of a better life for themselves an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5473925416613030952">(FROM JACKET)Six-year-old Clara Bynum is dead, drowned in the Potomac River in the shadow of an apparently haunted rock outcropping known locally as the Three Sisters.<br />In scenes alive with emotional truth, "River, Cross My Heart" weighs the effect of Clara's absence on the people she has left behind: her parents, Alice and Willie Bynum, torn between the old world of their rural North Carolina home and the new world of the city, to which they have moved in search of a better life for themselves and their children; the friends and relatives of the Bynum family in the Georgetown neighborhood they now call home; and, most especially, Clara's sister, twelve-year-old Johnnie Mae, who must come to terms with the powerful and confused emotions sparked by her sister's death as she struggles to decide and discover the kind of woman she will become.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DvZ9QP7f2WGdIpE+MK3hsd4kfzQ78OIW8jTarnRpZehrIkKTDA3NmxH65YVOzAcDl9O8LXItpbyYPd88uPyTrQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZdSbMI9yj8PE47Hdttz5K0ZfIPrhE3ei/CjtZHOUTc4AAKTjfaCbOUg7xWbIvR+ZD6jj46jOMAiWIej2vwG7iw==')})
  }

<a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91563.The_Measure_of_a_Man"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/books/1348762485m/91563.jpg" alt="The Measure of a Man: A Spiritual Autobiography " />

<script>
//&lt;![CDATA[

His body of work is arguable the most morally significant in cinematic history, and the power and influence of that work are indicative of the character of th&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText7534447636041522446\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;In this luminous memoir, a true American icon looks back on his celebrated life and career.  His body of work is arguable the most morally significant in cinematic history, and the power and influence of that work are indicative of the character of the man behind the many storied roles. Here, Sidney Poitier explores these elements of character and personal values to take his own measure - as a man, as a husband, and father, and as an actor.Poitier was uncompromising as he pursued a personal and public life that would honor his upbringing and the invaluable legacy of his parents. Committed to the notion that what one does for a living articulates who one is, Poitier played only forceful and affecting characters who said something positive, useful, and lasting about the human condition.Here, finally, is Poitier\'s own introspective look at what has informed his performances and his life. Poitier explores the nature of sacrifice and commitment, pride and humility, rage and forgiveness, and paying the price for artistic integrity. What emerges is a picture of a man seeking truth, passion, and balance in the face of limits his own and the world\'s. A triumph of the spirit, The Measure of a Man captures the essential Poitier.Author Biography: Sidney Poitier was the first and remains the only African American actor to win the Academy Award for Best Actor for his outstanding performance in Lilies of the Field in 1963, but he believes that will soon change, given the excellence of African-American talent in the industry today. He has starred in over forty films, directed nine, and written four. His landmark films include TheDefiant Ones, A Patch of Blue, Guess Who\'s Coming to Dinner, and To Sir, With Love. Among his many accolades, he has recently been selected as the thirty-sixth recipient of the Screen Actors Guild\'s highest honor, the Life Achievement Award for an outstanding career and humanitarian accomplishment.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"7534447636041522446\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; return false;\"&gt;...more&lt;\/a&gt;\n\n    &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n      &lt;div Just nineteen years old, he\'s marooned in the Pennsylvania backwoods caring for his three younger sisters, whose feelings about him range from stifling dependence to loathing. And once he develops an obsession with the sexy, melancholic mother of two living down the road, those Victoria\'s Secret catalogs just won\'t do the trick anymore. Marie\'s children are Alice, seventeen - involved with a troubled young priest; Norm, sixteen - hotheaded and idealistic; and Benjy, twelve - isolated and misunderstood, and so desperate for his mother\'s happiness that he hides the deadly truth only he knows about Duvall. Among a fascinating cast of characters we meet the children\'s alcoholic father, Sam Fermoyle, now living with his senile mother and embittered sister; Sam\'s meek brother-in-law, who makes anonymous &amp;quot;love&amp;quot; calls from the bathroom of his ailing appliance store; and the Klubock family, who - in complete contrast to the Fermoyles - live an orderly life in the perfect house next     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/96531.Breena_Clarke">About Breena Clarke</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/96531.Breena_Clarke"><img alt="96531" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1390839593p2/96531.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer17753361833938329373"> Breena Clarke's third novel, ANGELS MAKE THEIR HOPE HERE, will be published in July, 2014. Breena is the author of two historical novels set in the Georgetown neighborhood of Washington, D.C. Her debut novel, RIVER, CROSS MY HEART (1999) was an October 1999 Oprah Book Club selection. Clarke’s critically reviewed second novel, STAND THE STORM is set in mid-19th century Washington, D.C. and was cho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17753361833938329373">	Breena Clarke's third novel, ANGELS MAKE THEIR HOPE HERE, will be published in July, 2014. Breena is the author of two historical novels set in the Georgetown neighborhood of Washington, D.C. Her debut novel, RIVER, CROSS MY HEART (1999) was an October 1999 Oprah Book Club selection. Clarke’s critically reviewed second novel, STAND THE STORM is set in mid-19th century Washington, D.C. and was chosen by the Washington Post Book Review as one of 100 best for 2008. Breena Clarke is co-author with Glenda Dickerson of the play "Re/Membering Aunt Jemima: A Menstrual Show" and is an advisor to the board of A Room Of Her Own Foundation. She is on the fiction faculty of The Stonecoast MFA program at The University of Southern Maine. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17753361833938329373">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2174991-river-cross-my-heart">Quotes from River, Cross My H...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Only a teaspoon of self-pity, girl. Every day give yourself a teaspoonful, but only a teaspoonful. Fill it up full, but only once! Don’t let yourself have more. You can’t live off it. But just a bit of it is like a tonic.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/691567-only-a-teaspoon-of-self-pity-girl-every-day-give-yourself" class="actionLinkLite">10 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“But comes a time for a woman when she stops thinking of herself as a girl, as a person of possibilities. She starts looking at the plain facts of herself. Her body that’s become the body that she has and her habits becoming the habits that she’s written in stone. Her “haves” being the ones she’s got and maybe not getting anymore.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/691566-but-comes-a-time-for-a-woman-when-she-stops" class="actionLinkLite">2 likes</a>
